Since each subject represents some multiple of the average cost, the values of these isoratios will not necessarily correspond. The distribution of overall standard unit labor cost is given in Chart 11. Since the distribution of standard unit labor cost by industries is quite narrow, the average given is fairly representative. It will be noted that for most of the industries, standard unit labor cost is very close to $1.25. (See Table 14).

The variation in standard labor cost by industries is shown by the index of variation, i.e., the standard deviation divided by the mean, which is .029. This means that about two-thirds of the industries fall in the range 1.16 to 1.24.
The index of variation for regions is also very small (.012). For all industries combined, the regional values of the standard unit labor cost are:
New England
Middle Atlantic

South Atlantic East South Central 1.22 West South Central 1.22 Mountain Pacific
1.22
1.20
1.16
The smallness of these regional differences suggests that when we take all industries, regional differences in wages are largely offset by differences in productivity.
Summary. The concept of standard unit labor cost is useful because it is a measure of the "efficiency" of the labor input. It is defined as:
Standard Unit Labor Cost =
Total Payroll Value of Shipments
The average value of this index for all manufacturing industries in the United States in 1947 was 1.20. The variation by industry and by region was very small, indicating that wage differences are largely offset by productivity differences.
